Environmental life cycle assessment (LCA) of Hal 7 -
Evaluation of the methodological choices pertaining to
performing a building LCA
Master’s Thesis
Alberte Mai Lund (s151746) & Buket Tozan (s143928)
Technical University of Denmark, Department of Quantitative Sustainability Assessment,
Kongens Lyngby, Denmark
27th of June, 2020

FOREWORD
This Master’s thesis is the ﬁnal project of the Master of Science in Architectural Engineering conducted by Alberte
Mai Lund and Buket Tozan from January to June in the year of 2020. The thesis is conducted at the Department
of Quantitative Sustainability Assessment at the Technical University of Denmark (DTU) in collaboration with
Vandkunsten.
Firstly, we would like to show great gratitude towards our main supervisor Morten Ryberg from DTU. We thank
him for showing a great interest in our thesis by always being available and by taking time to advice us. We would
like to thank Jan Kauschen from Vandkunsten for showing interest in supervising and sharing knowledge with us.
A thanks to Salma Zavari from Vandkunsten for presenting the renovation project of Hal 7 and for providing data
for our thesis. We would also like to thank Lars Jørgensen from Egen Vinding & Datter and Lillian Olsen from
Hal 7 for providing information about Hal 7.
Lastly, we would like to show great appreciation to our friends and family for being supportive through the exciting
time of conducting our Master’s thesis.
Alberte Mai Lund
Buket Tozan

Page 1 of 137
==PAGE:12==
ABSTRACT
The main purpose of this study is to investigate methodological differences between approaches of allocating
environmental impacts between life cycle of building components where reuse or recycling are integrated. Using
Hal 7 in Roskilde as case building, this is done by combining three allocation methods; Method 1 Recycled-
content, Method 2 Substitution-with-material and Method 3 Equal-share with the Circular Footprint Formula,
which creates ﬁve life cycle assessments of Hal 7. Two additional analyses are conducted, where the ﬁrst analysis
investigates which impact categories should be included in future building LCAs, and the second investigates
whether it is environmentally preferable to renovate buildings with a short term perspective.
The study concludes that the allocation methods plays a signiﬁcant role in terms of the magnitude of the potential
environmental impact scores. The choice of the allocation factor applied in Methods 2 and 3, is found to be crucial
for the impact scores.
Moreover, it is found that Water consumption is a great contributor in two out of three areas of protection, and that
this impact category amongst Human non-carcinogenic toxicity, Human carcinogenic toxicity, Fine particulate
matter formation, Global warming and Fossil resource scarcity seems to have a great inﬂuence and should be
included in future building LCAs.
Finally, it is found that Hal 7 as a building with a short service life of 10 years is not environmentally preferable,
when compared to the SBi-references, unless the service life of Hal 7 is extended to 50 and 80 years. Though, it
is found that preserving the existing concrete structure can save a great deal of environmental impacts, and thus,
the short service life of 10 years can be seen as an environmental advantage.

==PAGE:13==
1
INTRODUCTION
1
INTRODUCTION
A tendency increasing in the construction sector is the implementation of circular practices and a focus on environ-
mentally friendly solutions where reuse and recycling are integrated in the design. In order to decide the degree of
circularity in a building project, a life cycle assessment becomes a beneﬁcial decision tool. However, introducing
building materials with reused or recycled content creates an issue regarding allocation of environmental impacts
between life cycles. How can one calculate the environmental impacts due to production of materials if resources
are derived from another product? And is it fair to credit for recycling as well as recovered energy in the End
of Life stage when actions of waste management are happening in the future? These questions are considered
in the aim of investigating different allocation approaches when modelling a life cycle assessment of a building.
In this study, a renovation project of Hal 7 located in Roskilde is used a as case building to investigate the issue
of allocation through a life cycle assessment. Hal 7 is characterised by its short service life of 10 years and the
integration of directly reused components and building materials with recycled content.
Three widely used allocation methods are introduced and described; Recycled-content, Substitution-with-material
and Equal-share. These allocation methods aim to solve the multifunctionality in Module D of a cradle-to-cradle
life cycle, which appears due to reuse, recycling and energy recovery. The Circular Footprint Formula (CFF) of
the Product Environmental Footprint method (PEF), developed by the European Union, is applied to the allocation
methods creating ﬁve possible alternatives of a LCA; Baseline, Sub A0.2, Sub A0.8, Sub VA and Eq Share.
LCAs in the Danish construction industry are conducted with the tool LCAbyg which include speciﬁc impact
categories presenting a predeﬁned environmental area of focus. The impact scores of the ﬁve LCAs are used to
asses which impact categories seem important and whether they should be included in future building LCAs.
The renovation of Hal 7 is based on a temporary use as the service life is limited to 10 years. Constructing on
a short term basis enables ﬂexibility in design when fulﬁlling a change in future needs. However, it is crucial to
consider environmental beneﬁts as well as consequences when constructing in a short term perspective, compared
to a traditional long term construction, if these solutions are to be implemented in buildings of the future.
The study seeks to state appropriate recommendations to be considered in the Danish construction industry when
conducting building LCAs as well as deciding future solutions with an environmental focus.

==PAGE:14==
2
BACKGROUND
2
BACKGROUND
This section describes the environmental considerations and practices in the Danish construction industry from
the perspective of an environmental life cycle assessment for a building. A potential development of the life cycle
assessment is introduced, when building components are reused, recycled and used for energy recovery.
2.1
An environmental focus in the construction industry
The construction industry has a global impact on the environment by being responsible for 39 % of the total carbon-
dioxide emissions (Adams et al., 2019). From a national perspective 20 % of the CO2 emissions in Denmark
come from the energy use of buildings, and the construction process and material production are responsible for
10 %. Furthermore, the construction waste in Denmark represents 35 % of the total generated waste, which could
otherwise be used as a resource in the production of new products. (Klimapartnerskaber, 2020) As a member of
the European Union (EU), Denmark must participate in the effort of reducing greenhouse gas emissions (GHG)
with 40 % by year 2030 compared to 1990 within EU. In a political statement from 2019 the Danish Government
presented a national objective to reduce the GHG emissions with 70 % in the period from 1990 to 2030 (Klima,
2019). It is of great interest to involve the building industry in the effort of reducing the national environmental
impacts. In order to deﬁne appropriate actions for change in buildings, calculations of environmental impacts can
be used as a decision tool. A way to do this is to conduct the quantitative sustainability assessment Life Cycle
Assessment (LCA).
This methodology is being used in greater extend in multiple industries, and in the construction industry it is used
to assess the total impact of a building and which life cycle stages have a great inﬂuence of the environmental
impacts throughout a buildings service life. Currently, life cycle assessment is mainly used in sustainable cer-
tiﬁcation systems for buildings in the construction industry, e.g. the DGNB certiﬁcation system (GBC, 2020).
Sometimes, the owner of a project uses the life cycle assessment as decision context to decide which materials to
integrate in the building design, though, this is not widely used in the Danish construction industry since it is not a
requirement in BR15. However, due to the great activity and demand in new buildings, the environmental impact
still needs to be reduced signiﬁcantly. This subject has been studied in several perspectives (Thormark, 2006).
The potential in saving energy when producing primary material compared to secondary material, the operational
energy use in buildings, and substituting materials with more environmentally friendly alternatives, are all subjects
that have been widely studied in LCAs.
Environmental impacts due to operational energy use of buildings has been an essential focus in the construction
industry and energy requirements has been regulated markedly. In 1961 the requirement for energy consumption
of new buildings was close to 360 kWh/m2/year, and according to the Danish building regulations class 2020 new
buildings must comply with energy consumption between 20-25 kWh/m2/year depending on the type of building
(Regeringen, 2014)(Traﬁk, 2018). Thus, the potential for further restrictions of energy consumption are limited
(Regeringen, 2014). There is a need to change the focus on the consumption from the perspective of operational
energy to the perspective of materials (Lærke, 2020).
Page 4 of 137
==PAGE:15==
2
BACKGROUND
A current subject in the construction industry is reducing the embodied energy of materials, however, it is equally
important to design buildings with a high reuse and recycling potential to extent the service life of materials
and components (Thormark, 2006). Moreover, the production of materials for the construction industry causes
a great environmental impact. Over the last century the material consumption of the construction industry has
increased with a factor of approx. 10 (Stephan & Athanassiadis, 2018). In a recent study, it was found that the
production of materials and manufacturing of building components in average correspond to 50% and 33% of the
total environmental impact for service life of 50 and 80 years, respectively (Zimmermann, Andersen, Kanafani,
& Birgisdottir, 2020). In order to reduce the environmental impacts, the focus on sustainable building design is
increasing, and the concept of the circular economy is being implemented in the construction industry. On the
basis of this and due to climate change, the choice of materials regarding the reuseability and the recyclability is
becoming more practiced, and it affects the way of designing buildings.
Currently, the implementation of a LCA in a construction process is a voluntarily action, as it is a part of the
voluntary sustainable program of buildings. However, in February 2020 the Danish Government presented the
possibility of integrating objectives from the voluntary program as requirements in the Danish building regulations,
when constructing new buildings. From experience and testing, it is the intention to assess, which objectives from
the program should be implemented as requirements in future regulations. To conduct a LCA of a building can
from 2023 be a requirement as one objective among others when a building is going to be constructed. (Andersen,
2020) Given that buildings in the future will become more circular, it seems important to implement methodologies
in LCAs of buildings, which consider the circularity of the materials.
Principles of the circular mentality, Reduce, Reuse, Refurbish and Recycle (4 R’s), have already been introduced
in the Danish construction industry in different ways. One of the mindsets behind the 4 R’s is to construct ﬂexible
solutions with the ability to adapt to the needs and requirements through time with the purpose of reducing the
consumption of resources as well as the impact on the environment. In 2013 a development project in Nyborg
called Mini-CO2-husene, was carried out with a focus on rethinking the design and construction of single-family
houses in Denmark based on reducing the CO2-emissions through the life cycle of a house. Six houses each
with a different approach was designed, one made by the principle of ﬂexibility, Det Foranderlige Hus. This
house was designed to fulﬁll the need for change in a family with the possibility of rethinking the arrangement
of rooms. By introducing ﬂexible solutions resources and time due to renovation, can potentially be reduced in
the life time of a building. Only smaller components are meant to be disassembled and reused for other purposes
inside the house. (Klies, 2013) Another concept when implementing ﬂexibility in buildings, is the possibility of
easily build and later disassembled components, when designing in a short term perspective. Especially when
creating solutions for accommodations, the need for temporary and quick solutions has been beneﬁcial. The main
component of the structure consist of reused shipping containers combined to create small villages functioning as
apartments. This type of construction can easily meet a change in demand for accommodation, while reducing the
use of resources and time in the construction phase. Furthermore, when creating solutions with portable shipping
containers, components can easily be moved to other locations and reused for other short term purpose. (“CPH
Village”, 2020) (“Container Living”, 2020)

==PAGE:16==

2.2
Life cycle modules of buildings
The life cycle assessment of the materials and products, which are integrated in building projects can, according
to (DS/EN15804, 2019), be divided into the modules A1-A3, A4-A5, B1-B7, C1-C4, and D shown in Figure
1. These modules, respectively, consider the product stage, construction process stage, use stage, end of life
stage, and lastly, the beneﬁts and loads beyond the system boundary. DS/EN 15978:2012 transfers these stages
to a building LCA, where the environmental impacts of a building throughout its service life can be evaluated
(DS/EN15643, 2011).
Materials can be retained in the end of life stage if they are assigned to a reuse or recycling process in Module D,
where they can be reintroduced to the supply chain as secondary materials. Doing this, the life cycle perspective
changes from cradle-to-grave to cradle-to-cradle as seen in Figure 1. This implies that the materials potentially
can have multiple life cycles, which occasions the implementation of Module D in the life cycle. However, this
follows a question regarding the allocation of the environmental impact of the reused or recycled material from its
multiple life cycles.
Figure 1: The life cycle stages and modules for a building LCA (Rasmussen et al., 2018).
2.3
Transitioning from cradle-to-grave to cradle-to-cradle
In the construction industry LCAs ordinarily include modules from cradle-to-grave (C2G), thus, the modules A1-
A3, A4-A5, B1-B6, and C1-C4 (Rasmussen et al., 2018). More speciﬁcally, the industry uses the LCAbyg soft-
ware developed by Statens Byggeforskningsinstitut (SBi, now BUILD), which includes modules A1-A3, B4, B6,
and C3-C4 (Zimmermann et al., 2017). LCAbyg is a calculation tool introduced in 2015 and is developed based
on the European standards DS/EN ISO 14040:2008, DS/EN ISO 14044:2008, EN 15643-2:2011, EN 15978:2011
and EN 15804:2012 all representing life cycle assessments of buildings and building materials. By excluding the
Module D, recycling, reuse and recovery is outside the system boundaries of the model. Thus, if actions regarding
Page 6 of 137
==PAGE:17==
2
BACKGROUND
Module D are integrated in a building project it can not be reﬂected in the LCA model when using LCAbyg. Only
the processes leading up to actions regarding reuse, recycling and recovery are accounted for in LCAbyg, e.g.
sorting and collection of waste (DS/EN15978, 2012).
As the construction industry becomes more interested in implementing reused or recycled materials in building
projects, the life cycle perspective can be transferred from the cradle-to-grave to the cradle-to-cradle (C2C). In
Figure 2, the border between cradle-to-grave and cradle-to-cradle scope is illustrated. This ﬁgure also illustrates
how the life cycle assessment are expanded to handle the reuse, recycling and recovery. This expansion of the
system creates room for integrating an allocation method in LCAbyg.
Figure 2: The border between the cradle-to-grave and cradle-to-cradle scope of a building LCA.
The preferred communicated impact used by companies and politicians regarding the construction industry is
CO2-emissions e.g. in (Klimapartnerskaber, 2020). However, LCAbyg introduces a broader environmental per-
spective by taking account for nine impact categories shown in Table 1. These form the basis of the environmental
assessment in Danish building projects today, including the environmental calculations required in the sustainable
certiﬁcation, DGNB (GBC, 2020). The calculation tool uses the German database ¨Okobau.dat, and the chosen im-
pact categories assessed in the LCA tool reﬂects the impact categories within this database as well as the categories
according to the European standards of sustainability of buildings (Birgisdottir & Rasmussen, 2015).
Table 1: The impact categories in LCAbyg used to present the environmental impacts of a building.

2.3.1
The multifunctional processes of Module D
When introducing Module D in the life cycle assessment multifunctional processes occur within this module.
A multifunctional process is a process which provides more than one function, thus, more than one outcome; a
primary and one or more secondary functions. One of the multifunctional processes in Module D is recycling.
More speciﬁcally, the recycling process acts as a waste treatment process for a discarded product, while at the
same time, providing a new material to be implemented in a next product, see Figure 2. A multifunctional process
providing more than one secondary function is waste incineration. This waste process (1) handles waste streams,
(2) produces electricity and (3) produces heat, where the outcomes (2) and (3) are the secondary functions of the
process. (Hauschild, Rosenbaum, & Olsen, 2018)
2.3.2
Handling multifunctional processes
According to the ISO 14044 solving multifunctional processes is done by using the ISO hierarchy containing three
approaches; subdivision, system expansion with substitution and allocation. The ISO 14044 suggests performing
subdivision before system expansion, and lastly allocation (EU, 2010)(ISO14044, 2008). If it is possible to sepa-
rate the multifunctional process into minor units between the primary and secondary function, Subdivision should
be applied. If not, the conventional or the most likely alternative way of producing the secondary function of a
process should be identiﬁed, which results in an expansion of the system. In practice approximately 75% of LCA
studies considering solid waste management systems apply system expansion when handling the environmental
impact from the secondary function of a process (Laurent et al., 2014). If none of these methods are applicable
allocation should be applied, however, system expansion can induce the need for allocating environmental im-
pacts. Normally, the allocation of the environmental impacts in multifunctional processes are shared between the
outputs based on the physical or economic relationship. (Hauschild et al., 2018) This can be applied to reuse and
recycling as well as recovery.
2.3.3
Allocation approaches for Module D
According to van der Harst et al. (2015) allocation is deﬁned as; ”The distribution of the inputs and outputs of a
process among the different multiple outputs (products) of that process” (van der Harst, Potting, & Kroeze, 2016).
Thus, how do one allocate the environmental impacts caused by the recycling process of building materials? And
which approach should be applied to achieve a representative result for this material?
When materials are either reused or recycled a change in inherent properties between the original and the subse-
quent product system can be present. This has to be handled more carefully, and according to ISO 14044, this can
be handled with modelling open-loop or closed-loop allocation procedures (ISO14044, 2008). When applying a
closed-loop method, it is assumed that the recycled material can replace the same virgin material derived from
a primary production. This recycled material can either be used in the production of the same product, or in a
subsequent system producing another product using a material with equal properties. The open-loop allocation
procedure distinguish between two possible scenarios; recycling of a material replacing the same virgin material,
but with a loss in quality, and recycling of a material replacing an alternative material as a consequence of changes
Page 8 of 137
==PAGE:19==
2
BACKGROUND
in the inherent properties (ISO14044, 2008). All three recycling loops can be applied to systems with only virgin
material as input or a combination of virgin and recycled material.
Although, multiple ways of modelling the allocation in recycling of materials have been studied in the last two
decades uncertainties in the methodological choices still remain (Gala, Raugei, & Fullana-i Palmer, 2015). The
decided allocation strategy may have strong inﬂuence on the found results in the life cycle impact assessment
(Gala et al., 2015). Thus, the freedom of choice of the LCA practitioner can lead to uncertainties due to e.g. the
deﬁned system boundaries, the functional unit (FU), and modelling approaches when solving multifunctionality.
This implies that when conducting a life cycle assessment uncertainties appear in many forms (Cherubini, Franco,
Zanghelini, & Soares, 2018), and this is especially the case when considering building materials as these have a
service life between 5-100 years, and are often composed by several materials. The allocation in terms of recycling
building components is, therefore, difﬁcult to base on realistic assumptions, as future recycling practices cannot
be foreseen.
2.3.4
Occurring consequences regarding recycling of materials
According to (Rigamonti, Niero, Haupt, Grosso, & Judl, 2018) a coefﬁcient quantifying the amount of primary
material substituted by one unit of waste material from the EoL stage can be determined by the following two
procedures; deﬁne the Point of Substitution (PoS) and consider a quality factor of the derived waste material.
The Point of Substitution refers to ”the exact point along the recycling chain where the material derived from
waste can be used in substitution of the corresponding primary material” (Rigamonti et al., 2018). The challenge
is to identify the PoS as it differs according to the type of product or material. For example, in the life cycle of
paper the PoS along the recycling chain is identiﬁed as pulp, since it replaces virgin pulp in the production of new
paper shown in Figure 3. If the recycled paper was considered a substitution for wood extraction, the system would
neglect the avoided processes along the transformation from wood to paper (Viau et al., 2020). A different PoS can
be found when recycling plastic polyethylene therephthalate (PET). PET requires sorting of polymer, grinding,
washing and drying before the PoS is reached, which takes places in the end of the recycling chain as PET
granulate shown in Figure 4 (Rigamonti et al., 2018). According to (Viau et al., 2020), waste material can never
substitute primary material since the waste requires a treatment process where sorting, splitting, decomposing or
transformation is needed before the derived waste material is considered a substitution for primary material. The
issue of PoS must be carefully managed in order to calculate the appropriate environmental impact of a product
system with recycling.
Figure 3: PoS along the recycling chain of paper (Rigamonti et al., 2018).
Page 9 of 137
==PAGE:20==
2
BACKGROUND
Figure 4: PoS along the recycling chain of PET plastic (Rigamonti et al., 2018).
A quality factor comparing properties of the recycled material with the properties of the substituted primary
material is essential when calculating the extent of substitution. The quality factor is material depended and can
be determined by the number of possible recycling loops, or the change in content of speciﬁc solutes in a material
(Rigamonti et al., 2018). For metals, such as aluminium and steel, the quality factor between secondary and
primary material are considered close to 1, whereas glass, plastic, and wood-based products cannot replace the
primary material with a quality factor equal to one. The quality of materials as well as technologies in recycling
processes are under continuous development leading to uncertainties in the quality factor for the future, moreover,
the temporal and geographical scope can be a challenge when predicting future scenarios (Rigamonti et al., 2018).
Only few studies have accounted for the potential decrease in the quality of materials during the recycling process
(Gala et al., 2015).

INTRODUCTION TO CASE STUDY
3
INTRODUCTION TO CASE STUDY
In the neighbourhood of Musicon located in Roskilde an empty industrial hall named Hal 7 has been out of use until
2017 where the building was turned into a temporary social institution for the citizens of Roskilde municipality.
Before the renovation, the hall only consisted of concrete elements and light concrete blocks forming the structure
of the hall, see Figure 5. The hall has a total area of 1786 m2, but only 1022 m2 is a part of the renovation.
Figure 5: Case building - Hal 7.
As a part of the project team, the Danish architectural company Vandkunsten has designed the new application
of the old hall with the approach of renovating it with solutions suitable for temporary installations of rooms. In
collaboration with Roskilde municipality, Vandkunsten has implemented the required facilities to fulﬁll the needed
functions of the place. The facilities of the institution consist of rooms for workshops, relaxing and socializing,
ofﬁces, physical activity, restrooms, and kitchens.
A main strategy of the design has been to divide the building in three different climate zones with different room
temperatures to reduce the amount of building materials as well as the use of operational energy. The plan view
with the three climate zones are illustrated in Figure 6. Climate zone 1 is an unheated area, Climate zone 2 has
an average room temperature of 17 ◦C heated by a rocket oven and Climate zone 3 has a room temperature of 21
◦C heated by radiators and convectors. Thus only rooms within Climate zone 3 has been thermally insulated to
maintain the temperature.
Furthermore, the renovation is characterized by the implementation of ﬂexible solutions when separating the hall
into smaller areas. Reused 40’ and 20’ shipping containers have been installed as a strategy to easily create separate
rooms in the hall. These rooms all belong to Climate zone 3 as shown in Figure 6. Similarly, storage rooms are
created by reused 8’ shipping containers placed in Climate zone 1. The installation of shipping containers creates
a ﬂexibility at the EoL stage where the containers can be reused again for another purpose at the same or another
location.
Page 11 of 137
==PAGE:22==
3
INTRODUCTION TO CASE STUDY
Figure 6: Plan view of Hal 7 divided by the three Climate zones.
The renovated building has a service life of 10 years whereafter the building site is incorporated in an urban
development of Musicon. Therefore, a strategy of reuse and easy disassembly of building components have been
integrated in the design. By reusing existing components in the building it is intended to minimise the economical
costs and environmental impacts. However, in order to fulﬁll the requirements of the Danish building regulations
2015, primary materials are used as well. It has not yet been clariﬁed whether the industrial hall of Hal 7 is reused
or demolished after 10 years, thus, an assumption of demolition has been made in this study in order to model the
EoL stage in the LCA. From Table 2 the main building components are listed separated in categories; Primary,
Secondary and Site components. Photographs illustrating the design of Hal 7 appear from Figure 7-12.
• Primary components: Components from primary production. These components can either consist of only
primary material or a combination of primary and secondary material.
• Secondary components: Directly reused components collected from suppliers or other building sites.
• Site components: Components directly reused from the existing industrial hall.

4
METHODS TO HANDLE RECYCLING
4
METHODS TO HANDLE RECYCLING
This section will go into detail with the applied allocation methods in the life cycle assessments of Hal 7. First,
three allocation methods developed to solve the multifunctionality of reuse, recycling, and recovery are introduced.
Then, based on the Product Environmental Footprint method, the Circular Footprint Formula is deﬁned. These
allocation methods and the Circular Footprint Formula is then combined, and applied to the case study introduced
in Section 3.
4.1
Allocation methods
When practicing the integration of recycling in LCA, two main concerns arise; How do one assign the recycling
efforts and the recycling beneﬁts between different product systems? And how does the model account for quality
loss of recycled material (van der Harst et al., 2016)? When acknowledging these two concerns, different methods
can be applied in order to evaluate the outcome of different approaches. Using different methods can potentially
result in a discrepancy between outcomes leading to less reliable conclusions of the LCA studies (van der Harst et
al., 2016). It is the intention of this study to comply with three widely used methods, denoted Methods 1, 2 and 3,
illustrating three approaches of allocating the environmental impacts in recycling to analyse the outcomes of each
method.
• Method 1: Recycled-content (Cut-off)
• Method 2: Substitution-with-material
• Method 3: Equal-share
Method 1, Recycled-content illustrated in Figure 13, is also known as the ”cut-off method”. This method only
considers environmental impacts associated with the product at issue, thus, in Figure 13 the environmental impact
directly caused by Product P in its current life cycle are taken into account. Potential environmental impacts arising
after its disposal are cut-off from the system boundaries. Therefore, the process of recycling the discarded Product
P as well as the credits arising from avoided production of primary material are outside the system boundaries.
The grey boxes indicate the processes neglected when applying this method, thus, it cuts off the environmental
impacts and credits. Moreover, the credits associated with the potential avoidance of primary energy production
are not credited for in this method. (van der Harst et al., 2016) Since the recycling process of Product P is cut-off,
the system do not distinguish between closed-loop and open-loop recycling. The secondary functions of the model
is outside the system boundaries thus system expansion is not needed when using the Recycled-content method.
Page 15 of 137
==PAGE:26==
4
METHODS TO HANDLE RECYCLING
Figure 13: Illustration of Method 1 Recycled-content. The illustration to the left shows the allocation approach when the material are recycled
in a closed-loop, while the illustration to the left shows the material in a open-loop recycling. Y represent the share of secondary input and X
represent the amount of secondary output.
Method 2, Substitution-with-material, is illustrated with Figure 14. This method accounts for environmental
impacts associated with the previous, current and potential next life cycle. In the manufacturing of Product P both
impacts due to primary and secondary material are accounted for. Contrary to Recycled-content, the substitution
method accounts for the impacts of recycling Product P, while receiving environmental credits from the discarded
material by accounting it as avoided production, which can be used as a resource in the same or a new life cycle.
In the perspective of other waste treatment processes, energy recovery is rewarded by receiving credits for avoided
production of primary energy in the EoL stage. (van der Harst et al., 2016) The credits to the system due to
recycling and recovery is indicated with the green boxes. This implies that system expansion is necessary to
handle the multifunctional processes of recycling and other waste treatments.
Figure 14 illustrates the method in a closed-loop and an open-loop respectively depending on the inherent proper-
ties of the recycled material.
Figure 14: Illustration of Method 2 Substitution-with-material. The illustration to the left shows the allocation approach when the material
are recycled in a closed-loop, while the illustration to the left shows the material in a open-loop recycling. Y represent the share of secondary
input and X represent the amount of secondary output.
Method 3, Equal-share, in Figure 15 is a compromise between Recycled-content and Substitution-with-material.
The product system when using Equal-share is similar to Substitution-with-material, however, the system allocates
the environmental impacts equally between the life cycle at issue and the potential next life cycle (van der Harst et
al., 2016). This is indicated with a factor of 0.5 when quantifying the secondary input Y and the secondary output
Page 16 of 137
==PAGE:27==
4
METHODS TO HANDLE RECYCLING
X. In addition, the secondary functions from recycling and energy recovery are handled with system expansion.
Figure 15 represents the closed-loop and open-loop recycling, respectively, for this method.
Figure 15: Illustration of Method 3 Equal-share. The illustration to the left shows the allocation approach when the material are recycled in
a closed-loop, while the illustration to the left shows the material in a open-loop recycling. Y represent the share of secondary input and X
represent the amount of secondary output.
4.2
PEF Method
The European Commission has conducted strategies for Europe to increase the resource productivity and to de-
couple economic growth from the use of resources, and the environmental impacts coming from this. One of the
strategies includes developing a more sustainable consumption and production, and in connection with this, the
European Commission has decided to establish a common methodological approach to assess, display and bench-
mark the environmental impacts of products and services based on the whole life cycle of these products and
systems. The methodology shall be used by the EU members and the private sectors. (Zampori, 2019) A method-
ology that supports this strategy is called the Product Environmental Footprint (PEF) method, and is deﬁned by
Organisation Environmental Footprint (OEF). The PEF method provides an approach to model the environmental
impacts caused by the input and output ﬂows of materials associated with a product during its life cycle. Thus, it
is a multi-criteria measure of the environmental impacts of a product. The considered system boundaries in a PEF
study follow the general supply chain, which includes raw material extraction, production, distribution, storage,
use, and the end of life stage. In the end of life stage, treatments such as reuse, recycling, energy recovery and
disposal are considered. (Allacker et al., 2014)
4.2.1
Circular Footprint Formula
The PEF method introduces an allocation methodology with the Circular Footprint Formula (CFF), where en-
vironmental impacts associated with every life cycle stage of a product can be allocated between the previous,
current, and next life cycle. The CFF is given in the equation below and a description of the parameters within
CFF can be found in Table 3.

To illustrate the studied life cycle stages, the CFF is divided into thematic Blocks denoted A, B, C, D, and E as
shown in the equation.
The environmental impacts from the input of material in the manufacturing stage is presented by Block A and
Block B. Here, Block A corresponds to the impacts from input of primary material, while Block B is the impacts
due to input of secondary material. Block C considers the output in terms of environmental impacts from the
recycling process in the EoL stage, as well as the credits from the avoided primary production of a material due to
recycling. The allocation of impacts between life cycles due to reuse and recycling can be found in Block B and C.
The environmental impact from potential energy recovery at the end of life stage is presented as Block D. In this
block, the credits from avoided production of energy are subtracted from the environmental impacts of incinerating
material. Block E considers the environmental impacts caused by the ﬁnal disposal of the remaining waste of the
product or if the product cannot be reused, recycled or recovered. The environmental impact calculated in each
block is summed to achieve the total environmental impact of a system, Etotal.
4.2.2
Introducing the allocation factor of CFF
The CFF includes several parameters to be determined, which can have an inﬂuence on the environmental impact,
Etotal. The deﬁnition of these parameters are listed in Table 3. The parameter of issue when distinguishing
between allocation approaches is the Factor A. The factor A in the CFF is an allocation factor describing the share
between life cycles of the material or product. Applying the factor A in a product system enables to calculate the
sharing of impacts and credits from recycling the primary material, therefore, which share is to be carried by the
secondary material. Two extreme approaches are usually considered when applying the factor A. The ﬁrst one
considers A = 0, which corresponds to the 0:100 approach. The second one considers A = 1, which is known as
the 100:0 approach. If A is equal to zero in the CFF, the system only accounts for the primary production of the
secondary input material in Block B. Looking at the recycling process within Block C with A equal to zero, the
system accounts for the whole impact from recycling while receiving the whole beneﬁt of all the recycled material.
(Marc-Andree et al, 2019)
On the contrary, setting A equal to one, the system will not receive any credit where materials are recycled, how-
ever, when secondary material are used as input, the recycling impacts are modelled. In the CFF it is recommended
to apply three situations of the allocation factor; A = 0.2, A = 0.5 and A = 0.8. These three situations of the factor
A aim at representing the market situation as described in the following (Marc-Andree et al, 2019):
Page 18 of 137
==PAGE:29==
4
METHODS TO HANDLE RECYCLING
• A = 0.2 The system is following the principle of 0:100 approach. The factor describes a higher demand for
the material than available. The provided secondary material is of high quality, and is considered as primary
material. Setting A = 0.2 is an approach, where the system stimulates a higher recycling output in Block C,
since the demand is present. Materials following this approach are metals, paper and glass. (Marc-Andree et
al, 2019)
• A = 0.8 The system is following the principle of 100:0 approach. Here, the primary materials are considered
being downcycled or non-applicable in products, thus, the market price and the quality drops, since the de-
mand for these materials are low. Due to the low price, this approach stimulates the use of these downcycled
materials in Block B. Typical materials following this approach are textiles. (Marc-Andree et al, 2019)
• A = 0.5 This is applicable when the market situation is considered being balanced, e.g. for plastics, or if the
market value is unknown. (Marc-Andree et al, 2019)
Another allocation factor applied in the CFF, is the parameter B, which is used for allocating energy recovery
processes and generated secondary energy between life cycles. In the CFF this allocation factor is by default equal
to zero (Zampori, 2019).
4.3
Applying the CFF to the allocation methods
Learning from the allocation approach of Method 1, 2, and 3 in Section 4.1 while establishing the theory of CFF
in Section 4.2.1, the aim is to connect the three methods to the equation of Block A, B, C, D, and E.
Method 1, Recycled-content, is considered as a simpliﬁed version of CFF since the system only accounts for
environmental impacts related to the product system at issue, see Method 1 in Table 4. As a consequence the
equation of Block B and C are modiﬁed to exclude an allocation factor A. The total environmental impact from
the secondary material input and output are only affected by the sharing factors, R1 and R2. Similarly, the equation
in Block D only represents the impact of handling waste to energy recovery, EER, with a sharing factor R3, since
the avoided primary energy production is not credited for, and therefore removed from the equation.
In the perspective of Method 2, Substitution-with-material, the equations in Block A, B, C, D, and E correspond
to the exact structure of CFF, see Method 2 in Table 4, where the previous, current and the subsequent product
system are considered. This implies that the environmental impact is allocated between the primary and secondary
production in Block B, while the credits from avoided primary production in Block C and in Block D are accounted
for. The allocation factor A depends on the speciﬁc material according to the market value. In this study the market
situation where A is either 0.2 or 0.8 is applied.
The equation in Block A, B, C, D, and E used in Method 3, Equal-share, follows the same structure as Method 2.
The only difference is found in the value of factor A, since the approach of Equal-share allocates the environmental
impact equally between product systems, thus, the factor A is equal to 0.5 as default.

4.4
Applying the allocation methods with CFF to components of Hal 7
The outlined equations in Table 4 attempting to combine Method 1, 2 and 3 with CFF can be further adjusted to
comply with the speciﬁc life cycle of the three components types; Primary, Secondary and Site. The adjustment
are illustrated with two approaches; (1) Recycled-content and (2) Substitution-with-material and Equal-share.
Since some of the installed components consist of more than one material, e.g. a window, a life cycle diagram
representing each material is modelled, i.e. a diagram for both the frame of the window and the glass pane. This
is indicated by a separation of the life cycle into three levels; material level, component level and waste level, see
Figure 16. The transformation from one level to another is illustrated with dashed lines.
Life cycle of components with the Recycled-content method
Primary components modelled with the Recycled-content method follow the principle of Figure 16, which illus-
trates how the parameters of the CFF can be applied to a material life cycle. This results in the equation of Primary
components in Table 5. As shown in Figure 16 the material level carries the environmental impact from primary
production, Block A, and secondary production, Block B. When applying the Recycled-content method, only an
environmental impact, Erecycled, from the recycled material in Block B are considered. Moreover, the environ-
mental impact from the virgin material from a previous life, Ev, is considered having zero impact on the system
when it is introduced as secondary material. As shown in Figure 16, the environmental impacts at waste level only
considers the impacts and neglects any potential credits arising from recycling and energy recovery, thus, E∗
v and
ESE are excluded from the life cycle.

5
LIFE CYCLE ASSESSMENT
5
LIFE CYCLE ASSESSMENT
The following section describes the goal and scope, the life cycle inventory analysis (LCI), and the life cycle
impact assessment (LCIA) of the LCAs of Hal 7. The found results in LCIA are interpreted as well.
5.1
Goal deﬁnition
The aim of this study is to apply Method 1, 2, and 3 to a life cycle assessment of Hal 7. In this study ﬁve models of
the allocation approaches are created. A description of each LCA is given in Table 8. The life cycle assessments are
conducted to investigate the consequences of handling reused and recycled components according to the allocation
approaches within Method 1, 2, and 3 given in Table 8. Thus, the life cycle assessments are used to illustrate how
the methodological choices within the methods have an inﬂuence on the ﬁnal environmental impacts. In addition
to this, the choice and the inﬂuence of the allocation factor is evaluated with Substitution-with-material, where
three scenarios with this method are conducted.
Secondly, the assessment will consider the signiﬁcance, and therefore, the choice of impact categories when
conducting LCAs of buildings. Based on this investigation, it is the intention to analyse whether the impact
categories integrated in LCAbyg are representative.
Finally, the potential environmental advantages or disadvantages when designing and constructing ﬂexible build-
ings with short service lives will be evaluated.
5.2
Scope deﬁnition
The ﬁve LCAs in Table 8 include the life cycle modules A1-A3, A4, B6, C2-C4 and D, which represent the cradle-
to-cradle scope. A comprehensive life cycle inventory model is carried out for the LCAs. Since the same building
is assessed, the LCI is modelled with identical processes within the life cycle modules. In order to compare results
from the life cycle assessments the functional unit represents the model of the allocation Method 1, 2 and 3. The
functional unit and the required reference ﬂow are shown in Table 8.
5.2.1
Life cycle inventory modelling framework and handling of multifunctional process
With the focus on identifying the environmental consequences between the three allocation methods, a conse-
quential LCI modelling framework is considered. The study does not only address the environmental impacts
attributed to the system of Hal 7, but seeks to describe the change of impacts caused by the differences in the
allocation approaches, when handling multinational processes in Module D (Hauschild et al., 2018). By applying
Method 1, 2 and 3 to the LCAs, the multifunctional processes arising in Module D are automatically handled with
both system expansion and allocation, as described in Section 4.

LIFE CYCLE ASSESSMENT
5.2.2
System boundaries
The system boundaries are shown in Figure 20. Each life cycle stage from cradle-to-cradle is included in the
boundaries. With the interest of connecting the life cycle of Hal 7 to the formula of CFF, Block A-D attempt to
represent the life cycle stages of the system boundaries.
The system is simpliﬁed by excluding Module A5 in the Construction stage and C1 in the EoL stage, since it has
not been possible to collect representative data for the construction and deconstruction processes.
The Product stage from A1-A3 is modelled to represent the manufacturing of components as well as the process
of transforming directly reused components into the desired shape and look suitable for the design of Hal 7.
As a consequence of lack of information regarding water usage, Module B7 is not considered. Due to the temporal
service life of 10 years the need for maintenance, reparation, replacements, and refurbishments is considered
to be limited, thus, Module B1-B5 are excluded from the system boundaries. The Use stage only carries the
environmental impact from the operational energy use, Module B6, of the building. This module is modelled
separately from the remaining modules, since it is not included in the CFF, and therefore, it is not associated with
a block as illustrated in Figure 20.
Since the ﬁve life cycle assessment of Hal 7 follow the same system boundaries, the results remain comparable.
The foreground system represents the processes speciﬁc to the system where data are collected and modelled on
ﬁrst-hand (Hauschild et al., 2018). With the goal of investigating the methodological choices of reuse, recycling
and recovery in a building LCA, the multifunctional processes in Module D are placed in the foreground system.
The background system is modelled using the database ecoinvent 3.6 LCI cut-off.
5.2.3
Geographical, technological and time-related representativeness
The data collection of the LCI seeks to represent a Danish perspective. This implies material composition, service
life and disposal routes of components. Product information from Danish suppliers is to the greatest extent possible
used as sources when modeling each component.
Disposal routes at the EoL stage are determined by statistical data from Danish waste management systems
through the website of Eurostat (EU, 2020). Using the latest statistics of waste management, it is possible to
identify rates of recycling, recovery and landﬁll applicable in Denmark.
The ecoinvent database version 3.6 is an extensive and widespread database. The database was released in Septem-
ber 2019 containing 2200 new processes and an update of 2500 processes (Ecoinvent, 2020). Thus, the database
is assumed to contain technological processes reﬂecting a temporal scope of present conditions for manufactur-
ing, disposal and recycling of products in the background system. The data input of the model regarding speciﬁc
processes of the database for manufacturing, transport and disposal aim to cover a geographical scope of Europe
as representative of the Danish conditions. As a consequence of limitations in the ecoinvent database, not every
process is found within an European scope. In those cases, a global average is chosen.
Page 27 of 137
==PAGE:38==
5
LIFE CYCLE ASSESSMENT
Figure 20: System boundaries of the life cycle assessments of Hal 7.
Page 28 of 137
==PAGE:39==
5
LIFE CYCLE ASSESSMENT
Operational energy use is modelled using the speciﬁc energy mix representing Danish energy production. This
allows the model to reﬂect the present technologies as well as the share when producing energy from renewable
and non-renewable sources in Denmark. Speciﬁc energy processes of the database used in the model attempt to
comply with a geographical scope of Denmark, however if not available, a European average is used.
5.2.4
Basis for life cycle impact assessment
The life cycle assessments are conducted using the software openLCA version 1.10.2. The impact assessment
method ReCiPe 2016 Midpoint (H) is used, where 18 impacts categories from Figure 21 are calculated. First,
the LCIA are calculated with midpoint impact categories in order to ensure a transparency of the impact results
when interpreting the ﬁve LCAs of this study. Furthermore, the LCAs are assessed with the LCIA method ReCiPe
2016 Endpoint (H) to calculate the contribution of the 18 midpoint impact scores to the three endpoint categories;
Human health, Natural environment and Resource scarcity. From Figure 21, the transformation from midpoint to
endpoint in the matter of damage pathways are shown.
Figure 21: An overview of the link between the midpoint impact categories of ReCiPe 2016, and the three areas of protection. The unit of the
impact categories are given in the parenthesis (Huijbregts et al., 2016).
Page 29 of 137
==PAGE:40==
5
LIFE CYCLE ASSESSMENT
5.3
Life cycle inventory
This section will describe the planning and collection of the data in the life cycle modules A1-A3, A4, B6, C2-
C4, and D of the life cycle assessments. Based on a qualitative assessment of uncertain data input, the basis for
the sensitivity and uncertainty analyses are presented. In the end, the approaches to analyse important impact
categories and the service life of buildings are introduced. In Appendix D, the data processes of the inventory of
the LCAs is given and the deﬁned parameters of the CFF for each component are shown in Appendix G.
5.3.1
Planning and collection of data per life cycle stage
Product stage, A1-A3
The architect, Vandkunsten, has provided documents from the tender phase in the form of drawings of the compo-
nents and materials. The data extracted from these documents is therefore not fully representative as changes in
design or choice of materials typically follow after the tendering phase of a construction project. The contractor
of the project, Egen Vinding & Datter, has provided information regarding the suppliers of the input materials.
Information regarding the building services are found through the municipality in Roskilde. By a personal visit to
Hal 7, the ﬁnal design of the building as well as the ﬁnal use of visible building components has been documented.
The transportation during the Product stage, Module A2, is included by selecting providers with the market for
option for processes in ecoinvent database. Since the authors of the LCA has no inﬂuence of the data in this stage,
it is modelled in the background system, see Figure 20.
Construction stage, A4
This stage considers transportation of Primary components to Hal 7 from each supplier. The modelled transporta-
tion distances are given in Appendix F. Moreover, for Secondary and Site components, transportation, cleaning
and modiﬁcation are modelled.
Operational energy use, B6
Through contact with the consultant engineer, Steensen Varming, it has been stated that no calculations of the
energy performance are conducted due to special conditions of Hal 7 where the building is partly unheated and
has a modiﬁed purpose of use. In order to fulﬁll the building regulations from 2015, the building envelope has
been designed accordingly in Climate zone 3. Thus, the operational energy use of the building is unknown, and
therefore assumed to be equal to the requirement in section 7.2.4.2 in BR15. According to section 7.2.4.2 in BR15
the required energy consumption corresponds to 41 + 1000/A kWh/m2/year (Traﬁk, 2015).
As a consequence of the three Climate zones in Figure 6, the energy consumption only reﬂects the heated areas of
Climate zone 3 which corresponds to 305 m2 of Hal 7. With an operational service life of 10 years, the composition
of the energy mix is likely to change over time with the tendency of replacing non-renewable resources with
renewable resources. The change in production of energy is based on the study made by COWI and Traﬁk- og
Byggestyrelsen (COWI, 2016), which attempts to predict the energy mix for electricity and heat in the years 2015,
2020 and 2025. This energy mix is used to conduct a dynamic model of the energy use of Hal 7. The scenarios
predicting the energy mix are given in Appendix I.
Page 30 of 137
==PAGE:41==
5
LIFE CYCLE ASSESSMENT
End of life stage, C2-C4
This stage considers transportation and waste processing with respect to recycling, incineration and landﬁll. With
the tool Affaldsregisteret the most likely waste treatment center, which the materials are sent to, is modelled in
Module C2, see Appendix F (Energistyrelsen, 2020).
To take into account potential losses of materials, when managing waste to recycling and recovery, every material
of the components are assumed produce waste sent to ﬁnal disposal, though with a variation in share.
The reuse-, recycling- and recovery potential, D
In connection with the recycling and incineration with energy recovery, the avoided production of primary material
and primary energy are modelled as a credit to the system.
In order to account for credits due to recycling, the PoS is identiﬁed. For every Primary, Secondary and Site
component a material speciﬁc PoS is found in order to expand each system. In Appendix B the PoS for each waste
material sent to recycling is illustrated.
The lower heating value, LHV, when modelling energy recovery, are based on an overall assumption for six groups
of materials; metals, inerts, glass, wood, paper and cardboard, and plastics (Christensen, 2011). These values are
given in Appendix G.
Due to the assumption that Hal 7 is being demolished in the year of 2027, the energy mix prediction for the year
2025 is used to model the energy recovery of the materials. The efﬁciency parameter, XER, appearing in CFF is
based on the study by COWI and Traﬁk- og Byggestyrelsen. Processes of heat and electricity production within
the ecoinvent database that include an efﬁciency factor have been adjusted with a factor so that the efﬁciency
parameters correspond to the ones given in the study (COWI, 2016). The speciﬁc factor of each efﬁciency is listed
in Appendix E.
5.3.2
Basis for sensitivity and uncertainty analyses
The robustness of the LCA results will be investigated by conducting a sensitivity and an uncertainty analyses. The
analysis is based on a qualitative assessment on parameters found to be uncertain by the levels of low, medium
and high. The identiﬁed uncertain parameters and the respective level of uncertainty are listed in Table 9 and
the description of the assessment for each parameter can be found in Appendix J. The calculations of sensitivity
consist of a perturbation analysis and an analysis of a relative change. These calculations are conducted to identify
which parameters the impact categories of the system are sensitive to. These result are implemented in Monte
Carlo simulations which calculate the uncertainties of the system. Finally, the robustness of the total impacts
scores of the LCAs will be considered by calculating the impact scores with another LCIA method.
Page 31 of 137
==PAGE:42==
5
LIFE CYCLE ASSESSMENT
Table 9: The level of uncertainty identiﬁed for parameters.
Parameter

Perturbation analysis
With a change of +/- 10 % in the input, the potential of the system being sensitive to a parameter is found by
calculating the normalised sensitivity coefﬁcient Si,n given in Equation 1 (Hauschild et al., 2018).
Si,n =
∆output
outputi,n
∆input
input
(1)
The change in input is given by ∆input and the resulting change in the environmental impact scores is denoted
∆output. The value outputi,n represents the total environmental impact for Hal 7 for LCAs i = 1..5. To identify if
the impact categories of ReCiPe 2016 (H), n = 1..18, are sensitive to a perturbation, the statements given below
are used. This analysis includes a perturbation of each of the 20 parameters listed in Table 9.
• The system is considered sensitive to a parameter if Si,n ≥0.5
• The system is considered sensitive to a parameter if the average of all Si,n ≥0.3
Page 32 of 137
==PAGE:43==
5
LIFE CYCLE ASSESSMENT
The Relative Change
Uncertainty of the model associated with discrete choices of speciﬁc processes in the ecoinvent database are also
present in the LCI. To test whether the LCAs are sensitive to the choice of the inventory, the Relative Change RCi,n
in the total impact scores of the LCAs are calculated with Equation 2.
RCi,n = Escenario,i,n −Ebase,i,n
Ebase,i,n
×100%
(2)
The impact due to the change in inventory is denoted Escenario,i,n and the total impact scores of the impact categories
n = 1..18 for the LCAs i = 1..5 are denoted Ebase,i,n. In this analysis, the system is assumed to be sensitive to a
change in the inventory if the relative change is ≥2 %. This analysis is conducted for the parameters Modeling of
ventilation unit and Modelling of paint where the relative change is calculated based on a change of process in the
LCI.
Monte Carlo simulations
The Monte Carlo (MC) simulation considers the predeﬁned uncertainties in the background system, described
with statistical distributions of the data, while at the same time, considering the uncertainty of the parameters in
the foreground system, which the system are sensitive to. The uncertainty of these parameters will be described
with statistical distributions, which will then be reﬂected with a 95% conﬁdence interval of the impact scores of
the LCAs. Thus, the MC corresponds to a quantitative analysis of uncertainties in the systems.
Basis for the robustness of LCIA method
To test the robustness of the results when using ReCiPe 2016 Midpoint (H), the total impact scores of the LCAs
will be calculated using the ILCD 2011 Midpoint+. The goal is to investigate if the two LCIAs support the same
conclusions of the LCAs conducted with Method 1, 2, and 3.
5.3.3
Basis for impact category analysis
The investigation of the importance and the inclusion of impact categories are done by calculating the share
of midpoint impact score in the total contribution to the endpoint areas of protection; Human health, Natural
environment and Resource scarcity. Based on the magnitude of the contributions to the endpoint, the impact
categories that seem to be important to include in future LCAs will be identiﬁed.
5.3.4
Basis for service life analysis
Two analyses are conducted to study whether it is environmentally beneﬁcial to construct ﬂexible buildings with
short service lives compared to long service lives. The environmental impact of Hal 7 is compared with a reference
value of traditional constructions having a service life of either 50 years or 80 years. The references are conducted
by SBi where the environmental impacts from 60 case buildings are calculated with LCAbyg. One should be aware
that the function, area and energy consumption of the 60 buildings are not identical to Hal 7, thus the environmental
impact is compared by kgCO2/m2/year. The SBi-references are listed in Table 10 (Zimmermann et al., 2020).
Global warming is the only comparable impact category as a consequence of different LCIA methods.

(1) The impact of Hal 7 with extended service life
The service life of Hal 7 are extended to operate in 50 and 80 years in order to answer: Which building type
seems to be environmentally preferable in a time frame of 50 and 80 years? As it can be seen in Table 10, the
SBi-references include Module B4, which is the life cycle module associated with the replacement of components
in the Use stage. In order to compare the impact of Hal 7 with the SBi-reference in a life time of 50 and 80 years,
Module B4 needs to be included in the system boundary of Hal 7 in this analysis. In Appendix K, the replacements
scenarios for the components in Hal 7 are given in the life time of 50 and 80 years.
When the total environmental impact for Global warming of Hal 7 is divided over m = 1..80 years, the difference
DIi,yr in the total environmental impact between the SBi-references and Hal 7 for 50 and 80 years can, respectively,

DIi,yr =

for LCAi where i = 1..5 and yr = 50 or 80 years

(2) The impact of Hal 7 when repeating the concept
When constructing Hal 7 with a temporary service life of 10 years, it can be expected that the site is used for
another building to be constructed and used for additional 10 years. If this is assumed to repeatedly be the case in
a time frame of 50 and 80 years, respectively, it can be investigated whether temporary buildings can be preferred
from an environmental perspective compared to a traditional construction with a service life up to 50 and 80 years.
The question arising from this analysis is: How many times can the concept of Hal 7 be repeated before exceeding
the impact of a traditional building?
It should be noted that the construction of potential temporary buildings in the future after Hal 7 are uncertain and
unpredictable, thus, the total impact of Hal 7 is used as a general reference to indicate the impact from a temporary
building designed with similar concepts as Hal 7. In this analysis the original LCI-model, Scenario 0, at EoL stage
are compared to two additional EoL-scenarios, Scenario 1 and Scenario 2.
In the LCI of Scenario 0 in the ﬁve LCAs it is assumed that every component sent to recycling is sent to a
waste management center, where the waste is sorted and treated to be implemented back in the production of new
components as secondary material.
It can be argued that components installed in Hal 7 still remain a high enough quality after 10 years of service
life in order to be installed in the same shape in another building. The two additional scenarios, Scenario 1 and
Page 34 of 137
==PAGE:45==
5
LIFE CYCLE ASSESSMENT
Scenario 2, occur by modifying the LCI of Block C, D and E.
Scenario 1 incorporates that components installed in Hal 7 are directly reused for a new purpose in another life
cycle. In this scenario the concrete structure is assumed to be demolished and recycled as originally modelled.
Scenario 2 is modelled similar to Scenario 1, however, in this scenario, it is assumed that the concrete structure is
directly reused as the installed components after 10 years. Thus, the concrete elements are retained to function as
a structure for another building in a next life cycle at the building site of Hal 7.
The modiﬁed CFF of the allocation Method 1, 2 and 3 applied to directly reused components in Scenario 1 and
Scenario 2 are listed in Table 11, see original equations of the CFF in Table 4. The recycling rate, R2, in Block C
is 100 % at the EoL stage, and Block D and E becomes equal to 0. For simplicity of the analysis within Scenario
1 and Scenario 2, it has been assumed that the process of disassembling the components as well as potential
treatments of components when reusing them, ErecyclingEoL, are neglected. Thus, Block C only consist of the
credits due to avoiding primary production of material in the next life cycle, E∗
v . Furthermore, it is assumed that
the quality factor of the reused components at EoL stage, QSout, are equal to 1 since they are assumed to replace
the same type of component in the next life cycle.

5.4
Life cycle impact assessment
This section will ﬁrstly present and comment on the midpoint impact scores calculated for the LCAs to identify
which LCAs cause the lowest and greatest impacts. Then, the contribution from the midpoint impact categories to
the endpoint areas of protection are presented and commented on.
5.4.1
Characterised midpoint impact scores
The deterministic results at midpoint level of the LCAs found with ReCiPe 2016 (H) are given in Table 12. The
magnitude of the impact scores are indicated with a color scale from light green being the lowest impact score to
dark green being the highest impact score. From Table 12 the lowest impact scores are found in Baseline in all
18 midpoint impact categories, while the greatest impact scores are found in Sub A0.2 in all 18 midpoint impact
categories. Considering results from Baseline and Sub A0.2, the differences in impact scores within the 18 impact
categories vary with a factor between 1.5 and 12.3, see Table 13.
Another tendency is found when considering the results for Sub A0.8, which indicate that the scores are the second
lowest midpoint impact scores in all 18 impact categories. Looking at the factor variations in Table 13 between
Baseline and Sub A0.8, the midpoint impact scores only vary with a factor between 1 and 3.7.
The greatest and lowest score between Eq Share and Sub VA varies between the 18 midpoint categories, as
Eq Share results in the highest score in; Fine particulate matter formation, Fossil resources, Global warming,
Land use, Mineral resources scarcity, Ozone formation, and Terrestrial acidiﬁcation. For the remaining 10 impact
categories, Sub VA is resulting in the greatest impact scores.
Furthermore, when only considering the three LCAs with Substitution-with-material, a tendency appearing due to
the allocation factor is seen in the impact scores, where Sub A0.2 results in the greatest impact scores, followed
by Sub VA, and Sub A0.8.
5.4.2
Characterised endpoint impact scores
The characterised endpoint impact scores are listed in Table 14, 15, and 16, and the magnitude of impacts are
represented with the color scale from light green being the lowest to dark green being the highest. The tendencies
seen in the midpoint scores are seen in the endpoint scores as well. Baseline is associated with the lowest endpoint
impact scores followed by Sub A0.8, and the greatest endpoint impact scores is found with Sub A0.2. When
looking at Sub VA and Eq Share, the variation in highest and lowest impact scores follows a pattern similar to
the one seen for the midpoint scores except for Fine particulate matter formation, which in this case, is lowest for
Eq Share. If one compares the sum of the impact scores between the two LCAs, it is seen that Eq Share results
in the lowest damage to Human health and Natural environment, while the lowest damage to Resource scarcity is
caused by Sub VA.

5.5
Interpretation
The goal of this section is to investigate and interpret the consequences in the impact scores of the LCAs due to the
chosen allocation method. First, the contribution from materials compared to operational energy are identiﬁed.
Then, in order to analyse the allocation method in more depth, the contribution from each block of the CFF
is interpreted. The results of the sensitivity and uncertainty analysis are presented and the robustness of LCIA
method is interpreted. Results regarding the importance of impact categories and the service life analysis are
presented.
5.5.1
Contribution from materials vs. operational energy
The deterministic impact scores given in Table 12 reﬂect the total impact score. It is of interest to look into
the relation between the impact from materials and operational energy use, see Figure 22, as a consequence of
different allocation methods. To illustrate the relation only the impact category Global Warming is presented. The
relation between material and operational energy use for all 18 impact categories can be found in Appendix M.
The ﬁgure reveals that the impacts from the life cycle of materials are the greatest contributor to the total impact
score in the LCAs. The energy consumption is identical for each LCA, however, the contribution to the total
impact score differs between them. The contribution from operational energy is 18%, 7%, 14%, 11%, and 10%
in Baseline, Sub A0.2, Sub A0.8, Sub VA, and Eq Share, respectively, for Global Warming. This is due to the
varying impact scores of Block A-E caused by the allocation approaches within Method 1, 2 and 3.
Figure 22: The total impact for each LCA showing the contribution from operational energy use and Block A-E for Hal 7.
5.5.2
Contribution from the Blocks of CFF
The life cycle of the materials consists of impacts from the Block A-E, thus, it is interesting to identify the contri-
bution of impacts from each block. The results are presented by the three component types; Primary, Secondary
and Site in Figure 23. The speciﬁc components installed in Hal 7 within Primary, Secondary and Site compo-
Page 39 of 137
==PAGE:50==
5
LIFE CYCLE ASSESSMENT
nents can be found in Table 2. The impact category Global warming is used to illustrate the tendencies within
each block. The same tendency can be found for the remaining 17 impact categories due to the same allocation
methods. As illustrated, the contribution of environmental impacts from each Block is dependent on the type
of component. This is due to the differences of materials within each component category and their respective
environmental impact from their life cycle. The speciﬁc impact scores for each block for the LCAs can be found
in Appendix N.
Contribution from Block A
The ﬁrst graph in Figure 23 represents the contribution from the Primary components. In this category the produc-
tion of primary material in components, Block A, results in the greatest environmental impact for Global warming
in the LCAs. It should be noticed that the impact from Block A is identical despite the allocation method, since
no allocation factor is included in the formula of this block, see Table 5, 6 and 7. The impact from Block A for
Secondary and Site components is equal to zero since the primary input of material is avoided when installing
directly reused components.
Contribution from Block B
Block B, the secondary material input, is found to be depended on the applied allocation method since a variation
of impact between the ﬁve LCAs is present in Figure 23. When focusing on the Primary components, the lowest
contribution of Block B is found in Baseline. This is due to the fact that impacts from secondary materials are
only associated with the process of recycling, Erecycled, while the potential impacts associated with the primary
production of the material from a previous life cycle, Ev,B, are neglected, see Table 5.
Considering the LCAs Sub A0.2, Sub A0.8, Sub VA and Eq Share, Block B from Primary components is the
second greatest contributor to Global warming. For Secondary components, Block B is the greatest contributor to
the total impact for Global warming, since no primary material are used as input. For Site components Block B
results in the highest impact in Sub A0.2 and Eq Share and the second highest in Sub A0.8 and Sub VA. Contrary
to Baseline, these four LCAs account for the primary production from the previous life cycle of a secondary
material. For Primary components this corresponds to Ev,A and Ev,B for Secondary and Site components.
The LCA resulting in the greatest contribution from Block B is found for Sub A0.2. This is due to the allocation
method Substitution-with- material where the impacts associated with the primary production of material from a
previous life cycle, Ev,B, is allocated with 80 % to the LCA of Hal 7.
The consequence from the methodological choice of the allocation factor in Substitution-with-material is also
reﬂected in the impact from Block B when looking at Sub A0.8 and Sub VA. In Sub A0.8 only 20 % of the
impact from the primary production of the secondary material is allocated to Hal 7.
When disregarding Baseline, Sub A0.2 and Sub A0.8 act as a maximum and minimum boundary of the impact
score. Thus, environmental impact of Eq Share from Block B will always deviate equally from the two boundaries.
This is due to the default allocation factor of Eq Share which lies between the allocation factor of Sub A0.2 and
Sub A0.8.
Page 40 of 137
==PAGE:51==
5
LIFE CYCLE ASSESSMENT
When applying a material speciﬁc A-factor to Substitution-with-material, the impact score from Block B will also
lie between Sub A0.2 and Sub A0.8, however, this impact score can vary since the material speciﬁc allocation
factor depends on the type of materials, as well as the choices made by the LCA practitioner.
Contribution from Block C
The dependency on the methodological approach is also seen in the contribution from Block C. The total impact
from Block C is only found to be positive in Baseline. This LCA of Hal 7 does not receive any credits when
recycling or reusing components in the EoL stage. The beneﬁts of recycling are allocated to the next life cycle
when reintroducing the recycled materials as input materials.
Looking at Sub A0.2, Sub A0.8, Sub VA and Eq Share the pattern of impacts from Block C is similar to Block
B, though with a negative sign. The total negative impact of Block C within the four LCAs indicate that the
credited environmental impact, E∗
v , is greater than the process of recycling in the EoL, ErecyclingEoL. The lower the
allocation factor, the more avoided material is credited for in a potential next life cycle, see Table 6 and 7. Thus, in
Sub A0.2 Block C credits for the greatest impact from the production of avoided material, which is then followed
by Sub VA, Eq Share and lastly Sub A0.8. This tendency of Block C can be seen for all three component types
in each LCA.
Contribution from Block D
The contribution from Block D is found to be greater in Baseline than in the four remaining LCAs, when con-
sidering Primary and Secondary components. This is due to calculations in Baseline where the potential energy
recovered, LHV ×XER ×ESE, from the incineration process is not credited for, see Table 5.
The calculation of Block D in Substitution-with-material is equal to the method of Equal-share and is not inﬂu-
enced by the respective values of the A-factor. This can be seen in Table 6 and 7. Moreover, the contribution from
Block D is positive, since the impact from the incineration process is greater than the potential avoided primary
energy.
As illustrated in Figure 23 Block D for Secondary components is found to be positive, though, with a minimal
inﬂuence in Sub A0.2, Sub A0.8, Sub VA and Eq Share, see Appendix N.
Looking at Site components, the impact from Block D in Baseline is lower than in Sub A0.2, Sub A0.8, Sub VA
and Eq Share. The Site components mainly consist of the concrete structures of Hal 7 as shown in Table 2. When
comparing Baseline with the remaining four LCAs, it indicates that mainly the process where concrete waste is
recycled, ErecyclingEoL, contributes to the total impact score of Block D. The additional contribution from Block D
in Sub A0.2, Sub A0.8, Sub VA and Eq Share might be caused by the Lower Heating Value (LHV) of -245 kJ/kg
of concrete, see Appendix G. This implies that for every kg of concrete being incinerated, it requires 245 kJ of
energy, therefore, the impact from the potential primary energy production, LHV ×XER ×ESE, no longer becomes
a credit to the system.
Page 41 of 137
==PAGE:52==
5
LIFE CYCLE ASSESSMENT
Contribution from Block E
Block E seems to have the lowest positive contribution to Global warming from each type of component. Similar
to Block A, the impact from Block E is also equal between the ﬁve LCAs due to the identical calculation method
in the allocation approaches, see Table 5, 7 and 6.
From the three graphs in Figure 23, it can be argued that Block B and C vary the most in terms of the contribution
to the environmental impact Global warming. For Block D a deviation of the impacts can only be found when
comparing Baseline with the four remaining LCAs. Furthermore, Block A and E does not seem to be inﬂuenced
by the methodological choices in the LCAs.
