5.5.3
Inclusion of impact categories in building LCAs
Based on the characterised impact scores at endpoint level from Table 14, 15 and 16 it is found interesting to
identify to what extent the 18 impact categories of LCIA method ReCiPe 2016 (H) are contributing to the areas of
protection.
First, the damage to Human health is considered which is inﬂuenced by eight impact categories, see Figure 24. The
impact category Water consumption seems to have a signiﬁcant inﬂuence on Human health, since the contribution
to the ﬁnal impact score lies between 50-70 % in the LCAs.
Moreover, it is seen that the contribution of the four impact categories Human non-carginogenic toxicity, Human
carcinogenic toxicity, Global warming, and Fine particulate matter formation inﬂuence the damage to Human
health. These four impact categories together represents between 30-47 % of the damage.
As for the remaining three impact categories; Stratospheric ozone depletion, Ozone formation and Ionising radia-
tion, the contribution to Human health in the LCAs seems to be insigniﬁcant, since the sum of the impact results in
a total share between 0.02-0.04 %. Thus, it is seen that ﬁve out of eight midpoint impact scores have a signiﬁcant
inﬂuence on the area of protection, while three impact categories do not.
In terms of the area of protection, Natural environment, the impact category having the greatest inﬂuence is also
Water consumption, since the contribution lies between 84-93 %, though, this impact category primarily damages
the terrestrial ecosystem. Global warming is also found signiﬁcant with a contribution between 5-11 % within the
LCAs. Once again the damage is mainly to Terrestrial ecosystem. The remaining eight impact categories each
contribute with less than 2 %, thus, these categories do not seem to damage the area of protection signiﬁcantly.
In terms of Resource scarcity only two impact categories can cause damage to this area of protection. However,
noting the scale of the y-axis, it is obvious that Fossil resource scarcity damages the area of protection the most,
since it causes 99.5-99.8 % of the damage.

5.5.4
Sensitivity and uncertainty analysis
The results of the sensitivity analyses are described in the following sections. The uncertain parameters tested in
the perturbation analysis are further investigated in the uncertainty analysis.
Perturbation analysis
The 20 parameters, which are identiﬁed as uncertain, have been tested in the perturbation analysis and the result
of the sensitivity analysis is listed in Table 17. The normalised sensitivity coefﬁcient of the 20 parameters can be
found in Appendix P.
It can be seen that the LCAs are sensitive to a perturbation of the parameters R2 and R3. Moreover, Sub A0.2,
Sub A0.8, Sub VA and Eq Share are sensitive to the quality factor, QSin, while Baseline do not consider this
quality factor. These parameters are by default a part of the CFF, which have an inﬂuence on Block B, C and D.

Monte Carlo simulations
Triangular distributions for each parameter in the perturbation analysis, which the systems are found sensitive
to, are assumed by deﬁning a minimum, maximum, and peak value. For R2 and R3, the mean of the triangular
distribution is assumed being equal to the initial values of R2 and R3 for each material group and the minimum an
maximum values are based on assumptions as well. The distribution of the quality factor, QSin, corresponds to a
speciﬁc study of material properties (Viau et al., 2020). The distributions are given in Appendix Q.
Unfortunately, due an error in the implementation of the latest version of the ecoinvent 3.6 cut-off database, the
Monte Carlo simulation are excluded from the study as the results are found not to represent the correct 95 %
conﬁdence interval of the LCAs (openLCA, 2020). The Monte Carlo simulations of the LCAs with the 95 %
conﬁdence intervals can be seen in Appendix R. As it can be seen seven out of 18 impact categories becomes
negative in the simulation, though, this should not be the case. Moreover, for some of the remaining impact scores
the original impact scores in Table 12 do not ﬁt into the 95 % conﬁdence intervals.
Robustness of results
In contrary to ReCiPe 2016 Midpoint (H) in Table 12, the impact scores of Baseline in Table 19 do not consistently
result in the lowest impact score, thus, this LCA does not necessarily provide the lowest impact as the lowest
impact score for Particulate matter is found in Sub A0.8. Though, it is seen that Sub A0.2 results in the greatest
impact scores despite the LCIA method.
For Eq Share and Sub VA the greatest impact score changes depending on the impact category which was also
the case when calculating with ReCiPe 2016 (H). Overall, the third and fourth greatest impact scores are found in
these two LCAs, since Eq Share scores the greatest impact in nine out of 16 impact categories, while Sub VA has
the greatest scores in the remaining seven categories.
The considered impact assessment methods, ILCD 2011 Midpoint+ and ReCiPe 2016 Midpoint (H), do not en-
tirely base the calculations on the same impact categories, thus, these two methods are not comparable on a 1:1
scale.

The robustness of the overall conclusion regarding the impact scores with ReCiPe 2016 Midpoint (H) does not
seem to be consistent when compared to the results found with ILCD 2011 Midpoint+.

5.5.5
Service life analysis
The two analyses regarding the service life of Hal 7 are presented in the following sections.
(1) The impact of Hal 7 with extended service life
The total impact score of Hal 7 at year 50 and 80 including Module B4 are given i Appendix L.1. By applying
Equation 3, the DIi,yr for each LCA has been found and illustrated with a graph, see Figure 25. When a graph
intersects with the x-axis, it corresponds to DIi,yr = 0.
From the perspective of 50 years it can be seen that Hal 7 results in a lower impact score for Global warming
compared to the SBi-reference. This is valid for all the LCAs, however, for Sub A0.2 the intersection of the x-axis
is exactly at year 50. Hal 7 seems to be environmentally preferable compared to the SBi-reference when extending
the service life to 50 years in Baseline, Sub A0.8, Sub VA and Eq Share.
When considering a service life of 80 years, a similar tendency is found in Baseline, Sub A0.8, Sub VA, and
Eq Share. Though, when extending the service life to 80 years, Sub A0.2 also seems to become environmentally
preferable compared to the SBi-reference, since the intersection with the x-axis occurs before the year 80.

(2) Repeating the concept of Hal 7
In Figure 26 Scenario 0 for 80 years are conducted and illustrated. The scenarios for 50 years are given in
Appendix L.2. The black horizontal line in the ﬁgure represents the boundary of impacts based on the SBi-
reference. In Scenario 0, the impact of Hal 7 exceeds the boundary if built eight times no matter the choice of
allocation method.
From the graph it can be found that the initiative of reusing the installed components directly, Scenario 1, does
not reduce the impact of Hal 7 enough to repeat a construction similar to Hal 7 eight times in a time frame of 80
years.
However, when directly reusing the components as well as preserving the concrete structure on the building site
at the EoL stage in Scenario 2, it seems to reduce the impact of Hal 7 enough in order to repeat the construction
eight times without exceeding the boundary of the SBi-reference.
The results found in Scenario 0, Scenario 1 and Scenario 2 imply similar conclusion when considering a 50 year
perspective.
If temporary constructions similar to Hal 7 are replacing the traditional buildings with a service life of either 50
or 80 years, it seems only to be environmentally beneﬁcial when components as well as the concrete structure are
directly reused.

6
DISCUSSION
6
DISCUSSION
Uncertainties regarding the life cycle inventory
Along the life cycle inventory analysis several assumptions have been made in order to complete the goal and
scope of the study. These choices can have consequences on the characterised impact score at midpoint and
endpoint level presented in Section 5.4. In the Construction stage and EoL stage the transportation is based on an
assumption of the most likely routes where components are collected from suppliers, Module A4, and waste are
delivered to a treatment center, Module C2. The transport distances for each component may not lead to impact
scores reﬂecting the actual damage to the environment. It was found that neither of the LCAs were sensitive to the
transportation, however, a change in the transportation load is assumed to affect the ﬁnal impact scores of Hal 7.
Furthermore, the assumed operational energy use given in Section 5.3 is expected not to correspond to the exact
energy consumption, and might not be representative for Hal 7. The contribution from Module B6 to the total
impact scores, seen in Figure 22, can therefore look different with a change in energy consumption. The total
impact score of Hal 7 is expected to change as well. However, it can be argued that a change in the energy
consumption will not have a signiﬁcantly inﬂuence since the LCAs are not found to be sensitive to a perturbation
of this parameter.
In addition, when modelling the energy consumption it has been assumed that 50 % originates from heat and
50 % from electricity. The share is expected to be far from the actual relation between heat and electricity in
the operation of a building. In order to approximate a realistic and representative energy consumption of Hal 7,
calculations of the energy performance according to BR15 is considered necessary.
Although the database of ecoinvent 3.6 cut-off is considered representative in terms of a technical and temporal
scope, the use of processes with European or global providers do not fully comply with Danish conditions. One
should be aware that manufacturing technologies as well as production of energy in Denmark may differ from
technologies in other countries. This can inﬂuence the environmental impacts and may create false conclusions
when comparing the LCAs with other studies. Moreover, the model of the ventilation unit and radiators were
considered uncertain to reﬂect the speciﬁc products installed in Hal 7 due to missing processes in the database.
From Appendix D is can be seen that the chosen process to represent these two components is market for steel,
low-alloyed, hot rolled — steel, low-alloyed, hot rolled — Cutoff, U - GLO. Since this process only considers
the production of steel, it can be expected that additional manufacturing processes are necessary to produce the
ﬁnal component. From information provided by the contractor Egen Vinding & Datter the paint used in Hal
7 is a natural product produced by Egen Vinding & Datter themselves. However, information on the speciﬁc
content of the paint has not been accessible and a process for water based paint, alkyd paint, white, without
solvent, in 60% solution state, are used instead. However, only surfaces within the kitchen are painted due to legal
requirements and neither of the LCAs seem to be sensitive to a perturbation as well as a change in the process of
the paint. Having this in mind, it should be mentioned that other parameters of the LCAs, which are not identiﬁed
in this study, can create uncertainties to the ﬁnal impacts scores. In the light of investigating the consequences
of allocation methods applied to the LCAs of Hal 7, the uncertainty of these parameters are not considered to
inﬂuence the ﬁnal conclusion.
Page 52 of 137
==PAGE:63==
6
DISCUSSION
Pros and cons due to methodological choices in allocation
The tendency according to (Zimmermann et al., 2020), where building materials to a greater extent are contributing
to the environmental impact of a building, is also found to be the case in the LCAs from Figure 22. However,
Figure 22 reveals that the choice of allocation method inﬂuences the conclusion on how large the contribution
from energy and materials are. When applying Recycled-content to the LCA of Hal 7 the materials seem to have
less inﬂuence on the total impact score compared to the LCAs of Substitution-with-material and Equal-share, yet,
the energy consumption as well as the building materials are identical in the LCAs.
The chosen allocation methodology in terms of reuse and recycling is found to be crucial for the LCA. This is clear
when looking at the characterised midpoint impact scores in Table 13. The difference in the results signiﬁcantly
depends on the chosen allocation method as well as the value of the allocation factor A of the CFF, determining
whether the impacts and credits should be allocated to the input side of recycled material, Block B, or the output
side of recycled material, Block C.
When applying Method 1, Recycled-content, only the impacts and credits associated with the product system at
issue are included in the calculations (Baseline). Thus, the system considers the actual use of the recycled material
in Block B, while avoiding predictions on how to handle recycling at the EoL stage in the future in Block C. One
can say that future scenarios of recycling is unpredictable, and assumptions of future practices are uncertain.
Doing this is considered a strength of this method. However, it can be argued that neglecting the quality factors
in Recycled-content, can result in the wrong use of the recycled material which could otherwise be implemented
in another product with greater value (van der Harst et al., 2016). Furthermore, due to the great contribution
from EV,B in Block B for LCAs with Substitution-with-material and Equal-share, it might seem unfair to neglect
primary production of secondary material.
The quality factors as well as the allocation factor are integrated in Method 2, Substitution-with-material, however,
the consequences of methodological choices regarding the A-factor is clearly indicated in Sub A0.2, Sub A0.8 and
Sub VA even though they are based on the same allocation method. Although the demand of the materials of the
components in Hal 7 might vary, an overall allocation factor has been assumed in Sub A0.2 and Sub A0.8. This is
therefore a generalisation of the market values of the materials. The choice of allocation factor applied in Sub VA
is an attempt to create a realistic relation between the market values of the different materials used in components.
A general issue of Substitution-with-material is the principle of crediting for avoided impacts in the system of
issue. The actual environmental impact of Hal 7 is not recognized, when future conditions are predicted by
crediting for environmental impacts (Frischknecht, 2010). For building components with a long service life, the
waste management is likely to change in a time frame of 5-100 years as well as the market situation of recycled
material in the future.
One might argue that Method 3, Equal-share, corresponds to a compromise between Recycled-content and Substitution-
with-material, however, given the assumption A = 0.5 for each material, the market value of each material is again
a generalisation of the actual situation for the future. Similar to Substitution-with-material, the principle of Equal-
share also accounts for the quality of recycled material regarding the replacement of primary material. Applying
Page 53 of 137
==PAGE:64==
6
DISCUSSION
these two allocation methods requires an extended data collection compared to Recycled-content in order to cover
the issue of quality in terms of physical properties of the material, prices on the market and alternative materials
available. Due to difﬁculties when collecting these additional data, the calculations may not reﬂect true conditions
(van der Harst et al., 2016).
The issue of crediting for actions happening at the EoL stage is also present in Block D, where Substitution-with-
material and Equal-share tries to predict the energy recovery of incinerated waste. Due to changes in technologies
of energy production in Denmark, the speciﬁc substituted sources for heat and electricity is uncertain to predict
for future scenarios. However, the energy recovery in this study has been modelled based on a study by (COWI,
2016), predicting the future energy mix according to the present tendencies and statistical data.
The allocation when handling multifunctional processes is in this study based on the value of materials from the
present market situation. Yet, allocating environmental impacts by physical relationships are also applicable in
a LCA. It may not be possible to deﬁne an allocation factor for a general purpose, though, when acknowledg-
ing a common value of the factor for building materials, it may create a broader understanding between LCA-
practitioners, and results regarding the LCIA would potentially become more comparable. Having said that, the
allocation factor needs to be constantly studied and evaluated due to the potential changes in future demand, supply
and physical properties of materials.
From the awareness engaged by applying the three allocation methods to a building LCA, it is not possible to tell
which of the ﬁve LCAs approaches reﬂect the actual environmental impacts. A high or low impact score does
not indicate a right or wrong result. Thus, the choice of allocation method as well as allocation factor A is still
up to the individual LCA-practitioner, however, learning about the consequences of methodological choices may
provide sufﬁcient knowledge in order to decide when modelling a LCA as well as communicating the results to
an LCA-qualiﬁed and the public.
Applying CFF to a building LCA
The CFF is a formula based on the PEF method aiming at quantifying the environmental impacts from the life
cycle of a product. When analysing a building, the formula must comply with several products having different
material compositions, properties and disposal routes. This implies that speciﬁc values of A, R1, R2, R3, QSin, QSout
and QP should be carefully collected, which creates a comprehensive LCI-model. According to (Zampori, 2019),
default values of each parameter can be found when using the so-called Annex C, containing approved values by
the Commission of PEF. Unfortunately, when modelling the CFF for a building, Annex C becomes inadequate to
cover data for every component installed in Hal 7 due to the limited scope of materials. Thus, missing values of A,
R1, R2, R3 have been collected elsewhere and may not comply with the speciﬁcity and quality as found in values
of Annex C.
From the perturbation analysis, it was found that the LCAs are sensitive to R2 and R3, however, one might argue
that wrong or insufﬁcient values of parameters R1, R2, R3 are not conﬂicting with the question about consequences
when selecting different allocation methods, since these values are identical in the CFF despite the method.
In terms of the allocation factor when looking at the impact scores from Sub A0.2 and Sub A0.8, the sensitivity
Page 54 of 137
==PAGE:65==
6
DISCUSSION
of impacts due to the A-factor are clearly indicated. In addition, it can be anticipated that calculation of Sub VA
could result in different impact scores if the material speciﬁc A-factors were chosen differently.
Furthermore, the quality factors, QSin, QSout and QP, in Annex C are speciﬁc to packaging materials. Yet, these
values has been applied to the CFF of building materials in this study. Having QSin too low, creates less impact
than the actual case in Block B. Assuming QSout too high, the LCA receives credits too high in Block C. From
the results of the perturbation analysis, the LCAs of Hal 7 was found to be sensitive to QSin. This implies that the
results of the LCIA as well as the derived conclusion could be different if another value of QSin was determined.
Having this in mind, the quality factors do not inﬂuence the conclusion when comparing LCAs with Substitution-
with-material with the LCA of Equal-share, since the same values are used. The uncertainty of the quality factors
becomes crucial when comparing these two methods with Recycled-content, since no quality factors are taken into
account in this method.
When crediting for recycling in Block C, it is found crucial to identify the exact PoS along the recycling chain.
Since the PoS for each waste material affects which processes are avoided when calculating E∗
v , it may require a
speciﬁc knowledge about techniques in manufacturing processes in order to detect how and when recycled material
is integrated. Consequently, accounting for the wrong resources and processes does not reﬂect the actual avoided
environmental impacts.
The values of LHV applied in Block D are determined according to (Christensen, 2011) which categorise the waste
material into six groups; metals, inerts, glass, wood, paper and cardboard and plastics. It has been assumed that
each material used in the construction of Hal 7 corresponds to one of these categories and implies a generalisation
of the LHV. Especially in the group of inerts, which in this study covers both concrete, light concrete, bricks, clay,
cement panels, gypsum, wood wool boards and sanitary ceramics. Furthermore, the LHV of paper and cardboard
is assumed to represent a LHV for cellulose ﬁbre insulation. Calculating material speciﬁc LHV with information
on the ash, moisture content and combustible solids of each material, the calculations of energy recovery in Block
D can be expected to become more representative when applying the CFF to building materials (Christensen,
2011).
From the perspective of the energy recovery an allocation factor, B, has been integrated in the formula, however,
according to (Zampori, 2019) this factor must by default be equal to zero. This implies that the entire impacts due
to incineration as well as credits from recovery are allocated to the system at issue. When allocating impacts and
credits from reuse and recycling between life cycles with different factors of A, it can be argued that this approach
is also essential for energy recovery where B > 0. If waste material is generating energy in one life cycle, Block
D, it may substitute primary energy in the production of materials in Block A or Block B. Thus, an additional part
in the CFF allocating the impacts and credits between input and output of energy is considered useful.
Although, the data availability are limited in terms of the parameters of the CFF, the formula is considered applica-
ble to building components and thus building LCAs. However, the LCA practitioner should take into account that
a comprehensive data collection is required i.e. information regarding the material composition of components,
the quality factors Q, lower heating values LHV, the share of recycled content R1, point of substitution etc.
Page 55 of 137
==PAGE:66==
6
DISCUSSION
Short term vs. long term buildings
When calculating the initial environmental impact score of Hal 7 it was assumed that the entire structure of the
industrial hall is going to be demolished where waste is sent to recycling, recovery and landﬁll. With the plan
to develop the area of Musicon, the concrete structure of Hal 7 is also likely to be preserved and used for a new
purpose. If this is the case, the initial impact scores listed in Table 12 do not reﬂect the actual circumstances
and when comparing Hal 7 with the SBi-reference it might create a misleading relation. From Figure 23 it was
found that the concrete structure in Site components results in a large contribution to Block D as a consequence
of the incineration process. If the structure of Hal 7 are preserved for another use after 10 years, Block D will
create 0 impact. Furthermore, the contribution from Block C and E will be neglected as well and the total impact
scores are expected to decrease. When analysing if a building like Hal 7 is environmentally preferable compared
to a traditional construction, Scenario 0 in Figure 26, it can be assumed that the impact score from the life cycle
of Hal 7 becomes low enough in order to repeat the construction ﬁve or eight times in a time frame of 50 and
80 years, respectively. Considering the results of Scenario 2 in Figure 26, this also indicates that Hal 7 can be
environmentally preferred when keeping the concrete structure. However, Scenario 2 also assumes every installed
component are directly reused. Directly reuse of materials and structures is expected to create less environmental
damage, yet, it can require more time and increase expenses regarding dismantling and waste processing at the
EoL stage. Therefore, direct reuse of materials and structures is not prioritized in a construction project.
Another issue of the service life analysis are the uncertainties regarding design and use of future constructions
at the building site of Hal 7. For simplicity, the impact score of Hal 7, within the LCAs, is used as a reference
for short term constructions. However, using these LCAs as references, assumes that the future constructions are
identical with Hal 7, requiring an existing concrete structure, installation of similar components, reused as well as
new, and the same operational energy use. Since Hal 7 is an institution offering room for speciﬁc activities, the
next building, might not have the same function and therefore, not the same design. Even though directly reuse
is implemented in short term constructions of the future, the material compositions of components are crucial in
the calculations of the environmental impact. One can not predict functions and design of future buildings and
the supply of reused components are also uncertain, however, the analysis reveals principles worth considering if
a transition from long term to short term constructions are to be progressed in terms of the environment.
Comparison with the SBi-references in the service life analysis
It can be argued whether the SBi-references are comparable with Hal 7 due to inequality in the system boundaries.
As mentioned, the SBi-references are conducted by LCAbyg which considers a life cycle from cradle-to-grave. By
excluding Module D, the calculation approach of handling beneﬁts of reuse, recycling and recovery creates incom-
parable results. However, with the application of Recycled-content to the LCA of Hal 7, results of Baseline can be
considered the most comparable LCA since the beneﬁts from recycling, reuse and recovery are neglected. Having
said that, the system boundaries of the SBi-references additionally exclude the transportation loads in Module A4
and Module C2, which are accounted for in LCAs of this study, thus, the total impact score of Hal 7 can not be
fully compared with the SBi-references. Moreover, the reference values reﬂect an average of residential, ofﬁce
and other buildings. Thus, the provided functions of the buildings are not equal. This diversity of the buildings can
cause different needs of materials as well as energy consumption which also creates less comparability in terms of
Page 56 of 137
==PAGE:67==
6
DISCUSSION
the LCAs of this study. Having this in mind, the SBi-references are working as an indicator of the relation between
a traditional construction and Hal 7. Thus, the comparison can be assumed to provide attention and knowledge
regarding short term constructions in the future.
When only focusing on Global warming in the service life analysis, the drawn conclusion are limited when gen-
eralising a relation between Hal 7 and the SBi-references. As a consequence of the two different calculation
methods, LCAbyg and ReCiPe 2016 (H), only the impact category Global warming is assumed to be calculated
on the same basis. However, in order to make a comprehensive comparison other impact categories must be
considered.
Inclusion of impact categories in LCAs of buildings
Selecting impact categories when conducting the LCIA can inﬂuence the interpretation of results as well as the
ﬁnal conclusion of the study. Excluding impact categories with high inﬂuence in a study can potentially create
weak conclusions in a comparative LCA. Moreover, analysing impact categories with a minor contribution may
become trivial.
From the interpretation of Figure 24, inﬂuential as well as insigniﬁcant impact categories were found in the per-
spective of Hal 7. In the light of Human health and Natural environment, it was found that Water consumption is
the the greatest contributor to the damage of these two areas of protection. According to DS/EN 15804:2012 for
environmental products declarations of building materials and DS/EN15878 considering environmental assess-
ment of buildings, this impact category is included in the standards of impact indicators as Water use measured
in m3. However, looking at Table 1, the consumption of water is not considered when calculating environmental
impacts with LCAbyg. Based on the great contribution from Water consumption in the LCAs of Hal 7, it can be
perceived as an important indicator for LCAs of buildings and it should be considered implemented in LCAbyg.
The damage to Human health was also found to be inﬂuenced by Human carcinogenic toxicity, Human non-
carcinogenic toxicity and Fine particulate matter formation. Nor are these three impact categories a part of the
LCIA method of LCAbyg. However, a common impact in focus between LCAbyg, DS/EN 15804, DS/EN15878
and ReCiPe 2016 (H) is Global warming expressed in kgCO2eq. CO2 is a widely used indicator of emission across
industries when focusing on climate change and along the increasing concern and awareness of this indicator,
Global warming can be considered relatable and manageable in the construction industry. If one compares the
contribution to Human health from Global warming with Human carcinogenic toxicity, Human non-carcinogenic
toxicity, and Fine particulate matter formation, it can be argued that these three impact categories should be
included in life cycle assessments of buildings as well, since they seem to contribute with a similar signiﬁcance
as Global warming does. However, introducing new impact categories in a LCA might conﬂict with the technical
level of knowledge when communicating studies to the respective target audience.
Even though the LCIA method ReCiPe 2016 (H) seems to cover a wide range of environmental concerns across
the 18 impact categories, it can be argued that including Stratospheric ozone depletion, Ozone formation and
Ionizing radiation causes a misleading focus when interpreting results of a building LCA due to the insigniﬁcant
inﬂuence to Human health. Given that Stratospheric ozone depletion is among the nine impact categories of
Page 57 of 137
==PAGE:68==
6
DISCUSSION
LCAbyg, the ﬁndings of this analysis indicates that this impact category can be replaced by Human carcinogenic
toxicity, Human non-carcinogenic toxicity, and Fine particulate matter formation with advantage.
The same issue is present regarding the contribution to Natural environment where eight out of 10 impact cate-
gories is found not to inﬂuence the damage to this area of protection particularly. With the small contribution from
Terrestrial acidiﬁcation found in Figure 24 it seems to have less importance to implement this indicator in LCAbyg
which corresponds to Acidiﬁcation of soil and water, see Table 1. Furthermore, results of impact categories con-
tributing to Resource scarcity reveals that only focusing on Fossil resource scarcity may be adequate for LCAs
of buildings. Having said that, it should be mentioned that impact categories having a small contribution to both
Human health, Natural environment and Resource scarcity are still damaging the environment to some degree,
yet, if actions are implemented due to results of a LCA, one can argue that only focusing on impact categories
with signiﬁcant inﬂuence creates greater changes of the environmental impacts. Lastly, it should be mentioned
that the inclusion of impact categories in future LCAs of buildings are based on the LCIA method ReCiPe 2016
(H) and Hal 7 as a case. Using other LCIA method as well as integrating multiple building types may provide a
different conclusion of which impact categories to include in future building LCAs.
Page 58 of 137
==PAGE:69==
7
CONCLUSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS
7
CONCLUSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS
In this study life cycle assessments from cradle-to-cradle have been conducted of a renovation project in Roskilde
named Hal 7. Through the learning about three overall methods, Recycled-content, Substitution-with-material
and Equal-share, the methodologies have been applied to the Circular Footprint formula creating ﬁve different
perspectives of the allocation methods, and thus ﬁve LCAs, when handling reuse, recycling and energy recovery
of building materials. From the LCIA of the study, the choice of allocation method was found to have an inﬂuence
on the total impact scores when calculating the environmental impacts with the LCIA method ReCiPe 2016 (H)
at midpoint and endpoint level. In Sub A0.2, Sub A0.8, Sub VA and Eq Share it was found that the choice
of allocation factor A, is also crucial for the impact scores. Baseline, based on Recycled-content, was found
to result in the lowest impact score in all 18 impact categories. The LCA Sub A0.2, where Substitution-with-
material with an allocation factor of 0.2 was applied, resulted generally in the greatest impact scores with a
factor variation between 1.5 and 12.3 compared to Baseline. When comparing Sub A0.2 with the two additional
LCAs of Substitution-with-material, Sub A0.8 and Sub VA, the importance of allocation factor A within the same
allocation method are clearly expressed by the signiﬁcant difference of impacts scores. The CFF is found to be
applicable to buildings, though, this requires a more comprehensive data collection. Though, the study of each
allocation method has created awareness of consequences between the methodological choices when allocating
environmental impacts, it is not possible to deﬁne a method to be applied in future LCAs of buildings.
Furthermore, it has been assessed that only six out of the 18 impact categories when using ReCiPe 2016 (H)
Endpoint seems to be important to include in the LCIA when conducting LCAs of buildings. Especially, the
impact category Water consumption, which is currently not an evaluated impact category in the tool of LCAbyg,
was found as the greatest contributor to the environmental damage of Human health and Natural environment.
Lastly, when comparing Hal 7 with references of traditional constructions, the principles and design of Hal 7 seem
to be environmentally preferable if the service life is extended from 10 years to 50 or 80 years. However, if Hal
7 is representing ﬂexible constructions with short term service lives, results showed that building components as
well as a potential concrete structure needs to be directly reused in order to comply with environmental impacts
from long term constructions.
Page 59 of 137
==PAGE:70==
7
CONCLUSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS
7.1
Recommendations
The LCAs are conducted to investigate potential consequences caused by the methodological choices in allocation.
Based on this analysis, the following recommendations can be stated.
1. Since it has not been possible to declare a correct allocation method, it should be considered to conduct
several building LCAs by applying multiple allocation methods.
2. When LCA-practitioners are applying an allocation method, it is recommended to carefully consider the
choice of allocation factor and to be aware of a potential difference in impact scores between methodological
choices.
3. If one applies the CFF, it is recommended to conduct a comprehensive data collection including material
speciﬁc information on building components.
When it comes to the importance and inclusion of impact categories in a building LCA, following recommenda-
tions are stated based on the ﬁndings in the analysis.
4. It is recommended to include impact categories Water consumption, Human non-carcinogenic toxicity, Hu-
man carcinogenic toxicity, Fine particulate matter formation, Global warming and Fossil resource scarcity
in the LCIA of future building LCAs.
5. To support the conclusion on which impact categories shall be included in a building LCA, it is recom-
mended to conduct additional studies based on multiple building types.
The service life analysis has brought recommendations for the Danish industry to consider regarding projects as
Hal 7 being environmentally preferable.
6. If a renovation project is based on a short term purpose, it is recommended to always consider preserving
the concrete structures.
7. To support the conclusion of the service life analysis, it is recommended to conduct studies that consider
other building types with different bearing structures.

I
Energy mix predictions

==PAGE:115==
J
QUALITATIVE ASSESSMENT OF UNCERTAINTIES
J
Qualitative assessment of uncertainties
This appendix contains the qualitative assessment of the parameters, which the LCAs might be sensitive to.
A perturbation analysis is conducted for each parameter, and a change in inventory choice of process for Ev for
paint and ventilation system. The normalised sensitivity coefﬁcient Si,n and the RCi,n are given in Appendix P.
Page 105 of 137
==PAGE:116==
J
QUALITATIVE ASSESSMENT OF UNCERTAINTIES
Parameter
Level of Uncertainty
Comment
Operational
energy
use
High
The industrial hall is initially unheated and changes in applica-
bility occurs where the hall is designed with different purposes.
The different use of the hall also affect the energy use which is
why no calculations of the exact energy use has been carried out
along the project. The strategy to comply with BR15 when de-
signing Hal 7 has been to fulﬁl the requirements regarding the
building envelope and building services of the building regula-
tion. The operational energy use in this study is based on the
maximum threshold of 30 kWh/m2/year to comply with BR15.
Energy mix
Low
The model of energy used for operational energy and energy re-
covery is composed by the energy sources representative in Den-
mark. The exact energy mix for district heat and electricity is
based on a study by COWI and Traﬁk- og Byggestyrelsen calcu-
lating new emission factors of the energy supply in Denmark. It
includes a prediction for future scenarios of the energy mix up
til 2050. The energy mix of this study follow these predictions
when modelling 10 years of energy use. However, as a conse-
quence of missing calculation for the exact energy consumption,
it has been assumed in this study that the energy amount of dis-
trict heat and electricity is equal.
Insulation
Medium
In Hal 7 two types of building insulation has been applied. From
drawing material cellulose ﬁbres has been used as thermal insu-
lation in slab and walls. From a site visit it was discovered that
ﬂax/linen are used as well to insulate the steel containers. As a
consequence of missing data of ﬂax insulation in the Ecoinvent
database, it has been assumed that only paper wool is used. Fur-
thermore, the insulation type of ventilation ducts does not appear
from the collected information and Rockwool is assumed.
Transport, A4
High
The transport load from Module A4 is assumed to be the dis-
tance from the manufacturing site of components to the site Hal
7 transported by trucks. The manufacturing site has been deter-
mined by the location of the suppliers and does not consider the
any potential resellers between the manufacturer and Hal 7. Fur-
thermore, it is unknown whether manufacturing of materials and
ﬁnal components takes place outside the national boarders or if
the transport also includes shipping and air transport.
Page 106 of 137
==PAGE:117==
J
QUALITATIVE ASSESSMENT OF UNCERTAINTIES
Transport,
C2,
for
landﬁll and incinera-
tion
Low
The transport load of construction waste send to landﬁll or incin-
eration is determined according to a document of construction
waste management in Roskilde municipality indicating a spe-
ciﬁc location of the incineration plant and landﬁll site.
Transport, C2, for re-
cycling
High
The transport of Module C2 when waste is send to recycling is
determined by the searching tool Affaldsregister developed by
Energistyrelsen. The tool is used to search for treatment cen-
ters handling waste for recycling in within Roskilde municipal-
ity. The tool suggests several possible treatment centers when
searching for each material group, thus, an assumption has been
made to select the most likely location by a judgement of the
LCA practitioners.
Recycling rate, R1
Medium
The rate of secondary input material, R1, is for Secondary com-
ponents and Site components given as 100 % recycling, and is
considered correct. For Primary components the rate of sec-
ondary input is an assumption based on research of the present
technologies to integrate recycled material in new products. The
speciﬁc R1-rate related to the components of Hal 7 can, there-
fore, differ from the actual combination of secondary and pri-
mary material.
Recycling and recov-
ery rate, R2 and R3
Medium
The recycling and recovery rate of material waste is from Euro-
stat, where European statistics on disposal routes for waste are
extracted. Thus, the speciﬁc rate representative in Denmark can
be different. Furthermore, the data of waste is separated into six
categories; Metal (ferrous), Metals (non-ferrous), Glass, Wood,
Plastics and Mineral. The R2 and R3 is therefore not speciﬁed
for a product but for a material group.
Quality factors, Q
High
The quality factor QSin and QSout are determined based on de-
fault values of CFF listed in an excel-ﬁle named Annex C, see
Appendix H. The default values of Annex C are speciﬁc to pack-
aging materials. The quality of building materials in a closed or
open-loop recycling is assumed to follow the values of Annex
C. This implies that the assumed quality of secondary input ma-
terial, and recycled output material may differ from the actual
quality factor which affects the calculations within Block B and
Block C.
Page 107 of 137
==PAGE:118==
J
QUALITATIVE ASSESSMENT OF UNCERTAINTIES
Avoided
material
production, E∗
v
High
The emission factor E∗
v is calculated based on the identiﬁcation
of PoS for each components sent to recycling. However, the
material at PoS modelled using Ecoinvent is considered highly
uncertain as a consequence of limited knowledge of what is in-
cluded in each process, and lack of data in Ecoinvent.
The
PoS of components made by different plastic types are all mod-
elled as polypropylene granulates because of missing data in the
database. Similarly, the process for PoS of glass is modelled
manually by combining the production of minerals used in glass
with an electricity load to turn solid glass into melted glass. As
for the PoS of gypsum and steel, the chosen processes in Ecoin-
vent are uncertain in terms of reﬂecting the actual avoided bur-
dens. The emissions factor E∗
v where gravel is identiﬁed as the
PoS is assumed to be representative.
Low Heating Value,
LHV
Medium
The values of LHV are determined according to the book
Solid Waste Technology & Management including knowledge
of the thermal treatment of incineration process and technolo-
gies. However, the LHV in this study is not calculated according
to the speciﬁc material, but has been categorized using general
values of six different types of waste listed in the the book; Plas-
tics, Paper and cardboard, Wood, Metals, Glass and Inerts.
Modelling OSB
High
The model of OSB has been separated into two processes; (1)
The wood chips and (2) Additives and energy for manufacturing.
The process for wood chips are available in Ecoinvent, however,
the process for additives and energy has been model by removing
input ﬂows related to wood chips in the process ’Oriented strand
board production — oriented strand board — Cutoff, S - RER’.
When applying this approach it is difﬁcult to detect which input
ﬂows of the process only relate to wood chips.
Modelling
cellulose
ﬁber insulation
High
The model of cellulose ﬁbre insulation has been separated into
two processes; (1) The newsprint and 2) Additives and energy
for manufacturing. The process for newsprint are available in
Ecoinvent, however, the process for additives and energy has
been model by removing input ﬂows related to newsprint in the
process ’Cellulose ﬁbre production, inclusive blowing in — cel-
lulose ﬁbre, inclusive blowing in — Cutoff, S - CH’. When ap-
plying this approach it is difﬁcult to detect which input ﬂows of
the process only relate to the newsprint.
Page 108 of 137
==PAGE:119==
J
QUALITATIVE ASSESSMENT OF UNCERTAINTIES
Modelling
ventila-
tion unit and choice
of process for Ev
High
The ventilation systems accessible in the Ecoinvent database is
uncertain to represent the speciﬁc ventilation unit installed in Hal
7 since the size and function are unclear. Based on material of
building services provided by Roskilde municipality the speciﬁc
type of ventilation unit is fond to be a centralized Exhausto unit
of steel with a weight of 190 kg. Thus the ventilation unit is
modelled by the process of manufacturing 190 kg of steel.
Modelling radiators,
convectors and dis-
trict heating unit
Medium
Due to lack of data on manufacturing of heating units in Ecoin-
vent, an assumption of steel manufacturing has been used. The
correct type of installed radiator, convector and district heating
unit are speciﬁed according to material of building services pro-
vided by Roskilde municipality which is used to determine the
amount of steel for each unit.
Modelling
and
choice of inventory
for paint, Ev
High
According to the contractor E&D the paint on walls and ceiling
is a natural paint produced by E&D themselves. Documentation
of the production of their paint has not been accessible, thus the
the LCI-model assumes a water based paint available in Ecoin-
vent. Since only walls and ceiling in the kitchen are painted, the
uncertainty caused by the impacts from the paint is considered
not to inﬂuence the ﬁnal results of the LCA signiﬁcantly.

S
CFF EQUATIONS FOR COMPONENTS
S
CFF Equations for components
This appendix describe the speciﬁc equation used to model each component of the three components types; Pri-
mary, Secondary and Site. Six tables are presented; one for waste due to renovation, one for Site components, one
for Secondary components, one for Primary components with recycled content and two for Primary components
only with primary input material.
